- Color legend: W=white, w=light gray, g=gray, G=dark gray, c=charcoal, B=black, M=magenta, P=pink, R=red, b=blue, S=sky blue, Y=yellow, O=orange, r=dark red, N=light green, p=purple.


Runtime variables inside every `python` tool call:
- `current_frame` is a lightweight frame view for the latest environment state.
- `current_frame` exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- `current_frame.ascii` is a single newline-delimited string containing the latest board rendered with the letter-coded ARC color symbols.
- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.
- The raw numeric grid is intentionally not exposed. Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, shapes, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes. Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region; do not scan the whole board with it.
- `history` is a chronological list of action/frame snapshots.
- `history` is a Python list of objects, not a dict.
- Each history entry exposes only `.action` and `.frame`; entries are not subscriptable like `entry['action']`.
- Each `history[i].frame` is the frame after `history[i].action`; each frame exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- Important history semantics: when `history` is non-empty, `history[-1].frame` is the same latest/post-action board as `current_frame`. It is not the previous board. To inspect the state before the latest action, use `previous_frame` or `history[-2].frame` when available.
- `previous_frame` is the frame before the most recent real environment action, or `None` if no previous frame is available.
- `last_action` is the most recent real environment action name/display, or `None` before any real action.
- `last_action_frame` is the post-action frame for `last_action`; it matches `current_frame` after a real action.
- `transitions` is a chronological list of actual action transitions, excluding the initial seeded frame. Each transition exposes `.action`, `.before_frame`, `.after_frame`, `.frame` (alias of `.after_frame`), and `.result`.
- `last_transition` is `transitions[-1]` or `None`. Its `.result` mirrors `last_action_result`; older transitions may have an empty `.result`. For before/after diffs, compare `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`; do not compare `current_frame` to `history[-1].frame`.
- `last_action_result` is the persisted result dict from the most recent `action(...)` call. It remains available across later Python inspection calls that do not call `action(...)`, and is `{}` before any action result exists. Read transition metadata from fields/keys such as `last_action_result['board_changed']`, `last_action_result['done']`, `last_action_result['level_completed']`, `last_action_result['game_over']`, `last_action_result['run_complete']`, `last_action_result['reward']`, and `last_action_result['valid_actions']`.
- `valid_actions` is the current list of valid action names.
- Call `action(actions)` to execute one or more real environment actions from Python.
- Pass `action(actions)` a list like `['LEFT']` or `[{'action': 'MOUSE', 'row': 4, 'col': 7}]`.
- One action usually returns one frame, but a single action can result in a short multi-frame animation.
- After `action(actions)` returns, `current_frame`, `previous_frame`, `history`, `transitions`, `valid_actions`, and `last_action_result` are refreshed.


Multimodal context:
- User turns include an attached image of the current ARC grid.
- The image and `current_frame.ascii` are two representations of the same current frame.
- You can use images and other tools to understand the game state and guide your strategy, each may be useful depending on the current uncertainty.


Visual-game guidance:
- Treat each board as a scene with objects, blockers, targets, adjacency, containment, motion, and symmetry.
- Game entities are usually be rendered as connected multi-tile shapes such as 2×2, 2×3, 3×3, or longer patterned structures. Sometime they might also be 1x1 tokens.- Some games are logic or layout puzzles with no explicit player avatar or controllable sprite on the board. Do not assume a player exists; the relevant state may be an object, region, cursor, selector, or whole-board configuration.
- Background colors are often white or gray/black-ish large regions, but not always. Verify background hypotheses by area, stability, and object boundaries rather than assuming them.
- In many games, a long horizontal or vertical line near an edge is a timer or remaining-steps bar. It often shrinks or changes each step. If you identify such a bar, do not get distracted by it or treat it as core gameplay state unless there is concrete evidence that it interacts with the puzzle mechanics.
A common failure mode is to mistake a segmented edge bar for clickable puzzle pieces. If a repeated strip of small blocks sits flush against the top, bottom, left, or right border and actions only change that strip while the interior board stays the same, classify it as HUD/timer state, not as an object to click through segment by segment. DON'T DO THIS!
- Use coordinates only to target actions or describe local evidence. Do not frame the objective as reaching a specific absolute row or column.
- Re-ground on the newest frame after any score increase or abrupt scene change; the returned board may already be the next level.
- `WIN` means the whole game is solved. Mid-run level completion is more likely to appear as a score increase while play continues.
- Strategies may transfer loosely across levels, but layouts and mechanics can change. Re-check the new board before repeating a plan.
- For `MOUSE`, pass `row` and `col` integer arguments. `row` is vertical position, `col` is horizontal position.


Python tool guidance:
- Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes.
- Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region of the board when `segmentation` is not enough; never use it to scan or summarize the whole board.
- Every `python` tool call starts fresh. Re-import modules or re-define any custom utility logic you need.
- The only importable standard-library modules are: bisect, collections, copy, fractions, functools, heapq, itertools, json, math, operator, random, re, statistics, string.
- The only tool is `python`; call it with one ephemeral `code` string.
- Always inspect `current_frame`, `history`, and `valid_actions` from Python instead of reasoning from the raw board by eye.
- For the most recent change, compare `previous_frame` to `current_frame`, or `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`. `history[-1].frame` is the current frame, so comparing it to `current_frame` only compares the board to itself.
- Maintain a compact working world model: what entities or regions exist, what actions seem to do, what the goal likely is, what remains uncertain, and what plan best fits the evidence so far.
- IMPORTANT: Especially when the game is about making an agent navigate to a target, it is usually safer to write an explicit search algorithm such as BFS. More generally, when the objective is understood but the best action order is unclear, pathfinding, flood fill, BFS, DFS, beam search, shortest-path search, limited action-sequence search, or custom heuristics are all valid.
- Optimize for the shortest reliable sequence that advances the current goal as described by your world model. If confidence is low, program a discriminating probe and revise the world model from the result.
- Once the important state variables and action effects are sufficiently understood, stop probing and search in the inferred state space.
- Inspect current and history frames from Python instead of describing frames freehand.
- Never print or echo full board frames. Return only compact derived summaries such as object lists, diffs, coordinates, counts, or tiny local crops.
- Keep tool-output context size minimal and decision-oriented so you can quickly compare before/after state. It's fine to write a lot of python code, just make the output short and interpretable
- A strong default loop is: summarize the board, infer the desired environment change, write a small scorer or search over candidate sequences, execute the best probe or plan with `action(...)`, then inspect again until you understand exactly what changed.
- For object tracking, match objects by color, overlap, bounding box proximity, area change, and edge contact rather than by exact coordinates alone.
- For frame diffs, summarize changed cells, color transitions, appearing/disappearing components, movement candidates, and small local row slices around the changed region.
- After every action, verify whether gameplay objects changed or whether only a timer, progress bar, or remaining-step bar moved. Do not treat HUD-only changes as evidence that the move worked.
- Use `print(...)` for compact summaries, or assign a final compact object to `result`.
- Call `action(...)` inside Python rather than returning action text in the chat.
- `action(...)` accepts an ordered list of one or more actions. Once your code has selected a reliable sequence, it is often useful to batch it.
- You can also call `action(...)` multiple times in one Python snippet, including inside loops. Each call updates the preloaded variables before execution continues.
- If an action result reports `game_over`, `run_complete`, `level_completed`, or `done`, stop acting immediately and re-ground on the next turn.

from collections import deque

# Player is at effective grid (0, 1) after the RIGHT move
# Goal is at effective grid (5, 5)

# BFS from (0, 1)
queue = deque([((0, 1), [])])
visited = {(0, 1)}
while queue:
    pos, path = queue.popleft()
    if pos == (5, 5):
        full_path = path + [pos]
        print(f"Path: {full_path}")
        directions = []
        for i in range(1, len(full_path)):
            dr = full_path[i][0] - full_path[i-1][0]
            dc = full_path[i][1] - full_path[i-1][1]
            if dr == -1: directions.append('UP')
            elif dr == 1: directions.append('DOWN')
            elif dc == -1: directions.append('LEFT')
            elif dc == 1: directions.append('RIGHT')
        print(f"Directions: {directions}")
        print(f"Length: {len(directions)}")
        break
    r, c = pos
    for dr, dc in [(-1, 0), (1, 0), (0, -1), (0, 1)]:
        nr, nc = r+dr, c+dc
        if (nr, nc) not in visited and 0 <= nr < 6 and 0 <= nc < 6:
            visited.add((nr, nc))
            queue.append(((nr, nc), path + [pos]))

# Execute the path
path_dirs = ['DOWN', 'DOWN', 'DOWN', 'DOWN', 'DOWN', 'RIGHT', 'RIGHT', 'RIGHT', 'RIGHT', 'RIGHT']
result = action(path_dirs)
print(f"\nResult: done={last_action_result.get('done')}, level_completed={last_action_result.get('level_completed')}, reward={last_action_result.get('reward')}")
</parameter>
</function>

Runtime variables inside every `python` tool call:
- `current_frame` is a lightweight frame view for the latest environment state.
- `current_frame` exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- `current_frame.ascii` is a single newline-delimited string containing the latest board rendered with the letter-coded ARC color symbols.
- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.
- The raw numeric grid is intentionally not exposed. Runtime variables inside every `python` tool call:
- `current_frame` is a lightweight frame view for the latest environment state.
- `current_frame` exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- `current_frame.ascii` is a single newline-delimited string containing the latest board rendered with the letter-coded ARC color symbols.
- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.
- The raw numeric grid is intentionally not exposed. Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, shapes, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes. Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region; do not scan the whole board with it.
- `history` is a chronological list of action/frame snapshots.
- `history` is a Python list of objects, not a dict.
- Each history entry exposes only `.action` and `.frame`; entries are not subscriptable like `entry['action']`.
- Each `history[i].frame` is the frame after `history[i].action`; each frame exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- Important history semantics: when `history` is non-empty, `history[-1].frame` is the same latest/post-action board as `current_frame`. It is not the previous board. To inspect the state before the latest action, use `previous_frame` or `history[-2].frame` when available.
- `previous_frame` is the frame before the most recent real environment action, or `None` if no previous frame is available.
- `last_action` is the most recent real environment action name/display, or `None` before any real action.
- `last_action_frame` is the post-action frame for `last_action`; it matches `current_frame` after a real action.
- `transitions` is a chronological list of actual action transitions, excluding the initial seeded frame. Each transition exposes `.action`, `.before_frame`, `.after_frame`, `.frame` (alias of `.after_frame`), and `.result`.
- `last_transition` is `transitions[-1]` or `None`. Its `.result` mirrors `last_action_result`; older transitions may have an empty `.result`. For before/after diffs, compare `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`; do not compare `current_frame` to `history[-1].frame`.
- `last_action_result` is the persisted result dict from the most recent `action(...)` call. It remains available across later Python inspection calls that do not call `action(...)`, and is `{}` before any action result exists. Read transition metadata from fields/keys such as `last_action_result['board_changed']`, `last_action_result['done']`, `last_action_result['level_completed']`, `last_action_result['game_over']`, `last_action_result['run_complete']`, `last_action_result['reward']`, and `last_action_result['valid_actions']`.

Visual-game guidance:

Do not assume a player exists; the relevant state may be an object, region, cursor, selector, or whole-board configuration.
- Background colors are often white or gray/black-ish large regions, but not always. Verify background hypotheses by area, stability, and object boundaries rather than assuming them.
- In many games, a long horizontal or vertical line near an edge is a timer or remaining-steps bar. It often shrinks or changes each step. If you identify such a bar, do not get distracted by it or treat it as core gameplay state unless there is concrete evidence that it interacts with the puzzle mechanics.
A common failure mode is to mistake a segmented edge bar for clickable puzzle pieces. If a repeated strip of small blocks sits flush against the top, bottom, left, or right border and actions only change that strip while the interior board stays the same, classify it as HUD/timer state, not as an object to click through segment by segment. Runtime variables inside every `python` tool call:
- `current_frame` is a lightweight frame view for the latest environment state.
- `current_frame` exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- `current_frame.ascii` is a single newline-delimited string containing the latest board rendered with the letter-coded ARC color symbols.
- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.
- The raw numeric grid is intentionally not exposed. Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, shapes, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes. Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region; do not scan the whole board with it.
- `history` is a chronological list of action/frame snapshots.
- `history` is a Python list of objects, not a dict.
- Each history entry exposes only `.action` and `.frame`; entries are not subscriptable like `entry['action']`.
- Each `history[i].frame` is the frame after `history[i].action`; each frame exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- Important history semantics: when `history` is non-empty, `history[-1].frame` is the same latest/post-action board as `current_frame`. It is not the previous board. To inspect the state before the latest action, use `previous_frame` or `history[-2].frame` when available.
- `previous_frame` is the frame before the most recent real environment action, or `None` if no previous frame is available.
- `last_action` is the most recent real environment action name/display, or `None` before any real action.
- `last_action_frame` is the post-action frame for `last_action`; it matches `current_frame` after a real action.
- `transitions` is a chronological list of actual action transitions, excluding the initial seeded frame. Each transition exposes `.action`, `.before_frame`, `.after_frame`, `.frame` (alias of `.after_frame`), and `.result`.
- `last_transition` is `transitions[-1]` or `None`. Its `.result` mirrors `last_action_result`; older transitions may have an empty `.result`. For before/after diffs, compare `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`; do not compare `current_frame` to `history[-1].frame`.

Runtime variables inside every `python` tool call:
- `current_frame` is a lightweight frame view for the latest environment state.
- `current_frame` exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- `current_frame.ascii` is a single newline-delimited string containing the latest board rendered with the letter-coded ARC color symbols.
- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.
- The raw numeric grid is intentionally not exposed. Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, shapes, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes. Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region; do not scan the whole board with it.
- `history` is a chronological list of action/frame snapshots.

- `last_action_frame` is the post-action frame for `last_action`; it matches `current_frame` after a real action.

Runtime variables inside every `python` tool call:
- `current_frame` is a lightweight frame view for the latest environment state.
- `current_frame` exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- `current_frame.ascii` is a single newline-delimited string containing the latest board rendered with the letter-coded ARC color symbols.
- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.
- The raw numeric grid is intentionally not exposed. Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, shapes, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes. Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region; do not scan the whole board with it.
- `history` is a chronological list of action/frame snapshots.
- `history` is a Python list of objects, not a dict.
- Each history entry exposes only `.action` and `.frame`; entries are not subscriptable like `entry['action']`.
- Each `history[i].frame` is the frame after `history[i].action`; each frame exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.

- `previous_frame` is the frame before the most recent real environment action, or `None` if no previous frame is available.

- `last_action_frame` is the post-action frame for `last_action`; it matches `current_frame` after a real action.

<parameter=code>
# Let's examine the segmentation to understand adjacency
seg = current_frame.segmentation
nodes = seg['nodes']
adj = seg['adjacency_list']

# Find all W nodes and their positions
w_nodes = []
for n in nodes:
    if n['color'] == 'W':
        b = n['boundary']
        r = (min(p[0] for p in b) + max(p[0] for p in b)) // 2
        c = (min(p[1] for p in b) + max(p[1] for p in b)) // 2
        w_nodes.append((n['id'], r, c, n['hash']))

# Sort by position
w_nodes.sort(key=lambda x: (x[1], x[2]))
print("W nodes (id, row, col, hash):")
for wn in w_nodes:
    print(f"  {wn}")

# Find adjacency between W nodes
print("\nAdjacency list (pairs of node ids):")
for a, b in adj:
    n1 = nodes[a]
    n2 = nodes[b]
    if n1['color'] == 'W' or n2['color'] == 'W':
        print(f"  {a}({n1['color']}) -- {b}({n2['color']})")

# Find player's current W node
player_r, player_c = 2, 4  # grid coords
pixel_r = 16 + player_r * 3
pixel_c = 16 + player_c * 3
print(f"\nPlayer pixel: ({pixel_r}, {pixel_c})")

# Find which W node is at player position
for nid, r, c, h in w_nodes:
    if r == pixel_r and c == pixel_c:
        print(f"Player is on W node {nid}")
        # Find adjacent nodes
        for a, b in adj:
            if a == nid:
                print(f"  Adjacent to node {b} ({nodes[b]['color']})")
            elif b == nid:
                print(f"  Adjacent to node {a} ({nodes[a]['color']})")
        break

Runtime variables inside every `python` tool call:
- `current_frame` is a lightweight frame view for the latest environment state.
- `current_frame` exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- `current_frame.ascii` is a single newline-delimited string containing the latest board rendered with the letter-coded ARC color symbols.
- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.
- The raw numeric grid is intentionally not exposed. Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, shapes, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes. Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region; do not scan the whole board with it.
- `history` is a chronological list of action/frame snapshots.
- `history` is a Python list of objects, not a dict.
- Each history entry exposes only `.action` and `.frame`; entries are not subscriptable like `entry['action']`.
- Each `history[i].frame` is the frame after `history[i].action`; each frame exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- Important history semantics: when `history` is non-empty, `history[-1].frame` is the same latest/post-action board as `current_frame`. It is not the previous board. To inspect the state before the latest action, use `previous_frame` or `history[-2].frame` when available.
- `previous_frame` is the frame before the most recent real environment action, or `None` if no previous frame is available.
- `last_action` is the most recent real environment action name/display, or `None` before any real action.
- `last_action_frame` is the post-action frame for `last_action`; it matches `current_frame` after a real action.
- `transitions` is a chronological list of actual action transitions, excluding the initial seeded frame. Each transition exposes `.action`, `.before_frame`, `.after_frame`, `.frame` (alias of `.after_frame`), and `.result`.
- `last_transition` is `transitions[-1]` or `None`. Its `.result` mirrors `last_action_result`; older transitions may have an empty `.result`. For before/after diffs, compare `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`; do not compare `current_frame` to `history[-1].frame`.
- `last_action_result` is the persisted result dict from the most recent `action(...)` call. It remains available across later Python inspection calls that do not call `action(...)`, and is `{}` before any action result exists. Read transition metadata from fields/keys such as `last_action_result['board_changed']`, `last_action_result['done']`, `last_action_result['level_completed']`, `last_action_result['game_over']`, `last_action_result['run_complete']`, `last_action_result['reward']`, and `last_action_result['valid_actions']`.

Visual-game guidance:
- Treat each board as a scene with objects, blockers, targets, adjacency, containment, motion, and symmetry.
- Game entities are usually be rendered as connected multi-tile shapes such as 2×2, 2×3, 3×3, or longer patterned structures. Sometime they might also be 1x1 tokens.- Some games are logic or layout puzzles with no explicit player avatar or controllable sprite on the board. Do not assume a player exists; the relevant state may be an object, region, cursor, selector, or whole-board configuration.
- Background colors are often white or gray/black-ish large regions, but not always. Verify background hypotheses by area, stability, and object boundaries rather than assuming them.
- In many games, a long horizontal or vertical line near an edge is a timer or remaining-steps bar. It often shrinks or changes each step. If you identify such a bar, do not get distracted by it or treat it as core gameplay state unless there is concrete evidence that it interacts with the puzzle mechanics.
A common failure mode is to mistake a segmented edge bar for clickable puzzle pieces. If a repeated strip of small blocks sits flush against the top, bottom, left, or right border and actions only change that strip while the interior board stays the same, classify it as HUD/timer state, not as an object to click through segment by segment. DON'T DO THIS!
- Use coordinates only to target actions or describe local evidence. Do not frame the objective as reaching a specific absolute row or column.
- Re-ground on the newest frame after any score increase or abrupt scene change; the returned board may already be the next level.
- `WIN` means the whole game is solved. Mid-run level completion is more likely to appear as a score increase while play continues.
- Strategies may transfer loosely across levels, but layouts and mechanics can change. Re-check the new board before repeating a plan.
- For `MOUSE`, pass `row` and `col` integer arguments. `row` is vertical position, `col` is horizontal position.


Python tool guidance:
- Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes.
- Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region of the board when `segmentation` is not enough; never use it to scan or summarize the whole board.
- Every `python` tool call starts fresh. Re-import modules or re-define any custom utility logic you need.
- The only importable standard-library modules are: bisect, collections, copy, fractions, functools, heapq, itertools, json, math, operator, random, re, statistics, string.
- The only tool is `python`; call it with one ephemeral `code` string.
- Always inspect `current_frame`, `history`, and `valid_actions` from Python instead of reasoning from the raw board by eye.
- For the most recent change, compare `previous_frame` to `current_frame`, or `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`. `history[-1].frame` is the current frame, so comparing it to `current_frame` only compares the board to itself.
- Maintain a compact working world model: what entities or regions exist, what actions seem to do, what the goal likely is, what remains uncertain, and what plan best fits the evidence so far.
- IMPORTANT: Especially when the game is about making an agent navigate to a target, it is usually safer to write an explicit search algorithm such as BFS. More generally, when the objective is understood but the best action order is unclear, pathfinding, flood fill, BFS, DFS, beam search, shortest-path search, limited action-sequence search, or custom heuristics are all valid.
- Optimize for the shortest reliable sequence that advances the current goal as described by your world model. If confidence is low, program a discriminating probe and revise the world model from the result.

    for a, b in adj:
        if a == g_id and nodes[b]['color'] == 'W':
            adjacent_w.append(b)
        elif b == g_id and nodes[a]['color'] == 'W':
            adjacent_w.append(a)
    # Connect all adjacent W nodes
    for i in range(len(adjacent_w)):
        for j in range(i+1, len(adjacent_w)):
            w_adj[adjacent_w[i]].append(adjacent_w[j])
            w_adj[adjacent_w[j]].append(adjacent_w[i])

# Find which W node is closest to player
player_r, player_c = 22, 28
closest_w = None
closest_dist = float('inf')
for w in w_nodes:
    b = w['boundary']
    wr = (min(p[0] for p in b) + max(p[0] for p in b)) // 2
    wc = (min(p[1] for p in b) + max(p[1] for p in b)) // 2
    dist = abs(wr - player_r) + abs(wc - player_c)
    if dist < closest_dist:
        closest_dist = dist
        closest_w = w

print(f"\nClosest W node to player: {closest_w['id']} at pixel ({(min(p[0] for p in closest_w['boundary']) + max(p[0] for p in closest_w['boundary'])) // 2}, {(min(p[1] for p in closest_w['boundary']) + max(p[1] for p in closest_w['boundary'])) // 2})")
print(f"Distance: {closest_dist}")

# Find which W node is closest to goal
if goal_node:
    goal_r = (min(p[0] for p in goal_node['boundary']) + max(p[0] for p in goal_node['boundary'])) // 2
    goal_c = (min(p[1] for p in goal_node['boundary']) + max(p[1] for p in goal_node['boundary'])) // 2
    closest_w_to_goal = None
    closest_dist_goal = float('inf')
    for w in w_nodes:
        b = w['boundary']
        wr = (min(p[0] for p in b) + max(p[0] for p in b)) // 2
        wc = (min(p[1] for p in b) + max(p[1] for p in b)) // 2
        dist = abs(wr - goal_r) + abs(wc - goal_c)
        if dist < closest_dist_goal:
            closest_dist_goal = dist
            closest_w_to_goal = w
    print(f"Closest W node to goal: {closest_w_to_goal['id']} at pixel ({(min(p[0] for p in closest_w_to_goal['boundary']) + max(p[0] for p in closest_w_to_goal['boundary'])) // 2}, {(min(p[1] for p in closest_w_to_goal['boundary']) + max(p[1] for p in closest_w_to_goal['boundary'])) // 2})")

<parameter=code>
import collections

seg = current_frame.segmentation
nodes = seg['nodes']
adj = seg['adjacency_list']

# Build graph: W nodes (and player) connected through g nodes
# Nodes: W nodes + player (14) + goal (66)
# Edges: through g nodes

# Find all nodes
w_node_ids = [n['id'] for n in nodes if n['color'] == 'W']
g_node_ids = [n['id'] for n in nodes if n['color'] == 'g']
player_id = 14
goal_id = 66

# Build adjacency: for each g node, connect adjacent non-g nodes
graph = collections.defaultdict(set)
for g_id in g_node_ids:
    neighbors = []
    for a, b in adj:
        if a == g_id:
            if b != g_id and nodes[b]['color'] != 'g':
                neighbors.append(b)
        elif b == g_id:
            if a != g_id and nodes[a]['color'] != 'g':
                neighbors.append(a)
    # Connect all neighbors through this g node
    for i in range(len(neighbors)):
        for j in range(i+1, len(neighbors)):
            graph[neighbors[i]].add(neighbors[j])
            graph[neighbors[j]].add(neighbors[i])

# Print graph for player and nearby nodes
print("Graph edges involving player (14):")
for neighbor in graph[14]:
    n = nodes[neighbor]
    b = n['boundary']
    r = (min(p[0] for p in b) + max(p[0] for p in b)) // 2
    c = (min(p[1] for p in b) + max(p[1] for p in b)) // 2
    print(f"  14 -- {neighbor} ({n['color']}) at ({r}, {c})")

# BFS from player to goal
queue = collections.deque([(14, [14])])
visited = {14}
found_path = None

while queue:
    node, path = queue.popleft()
    if node == goal_id:
        found_path = path
        break
    for neighbor in graph[node]:
        if neighbor not in visited:
            visited.add(neighbor)
            queue.append((neighbor, path + [neighbor]))

if found_path:
    print(f"\nPath found: {found_path}")
    print("Path details:")
    for nid in found_path:
        n = nodes[nid]
        b = n['boundary']
        r = (min(p[0] for p in b) + max(p[0] for p in b)) // 2
        c = (min(p[1] for p in b) + max(p[1] for p in b)) // 2
        print(f"  Node {nid} ({n['color']}) at ({r}, {c})")
else:
    print("\nNo path found!")

# Check if goal is connected
print(f"\nGoal node {goal_id} neighbors: {graph[goal_id]}")
for nid in graph[goal_id]:
    n = nodes[nid]
    print(f"  {nid} ({n['color']})")

Treat each turn as one observe-plan-act cycle: re-understand the current state from the newest frame, update your working world model in Python, choose the next best action or short sequence against the goal as currently understood, execute it, and expect to re-evaluate on the next turn from the updated state.
- Your job is to solve the entire game by clearing every level, not just the current screen.
- Levels often build on earlier mechanics, but layouts and interactions can still change between levels.
- Optimize for as few in-game actions as possible while still being reliable.
- In this environment, boards are presented as 64 x 64 color grids rendered with ARC color symbols.
- Color legend: W=white, w=light gray, g=gray, G=dark gray, c=charcoal, B=black, M=magenta, P=pink, R=red, b=blue, S=sky blue, Y=yellow, O=orange, r=dark red, N=light green, p=purple.


Runtime variables inside every `python` tool call:
- `current_frame` is a lightweight frame view for the latest environment state.
- `current_frame` exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- `current_frame.ascii` is a single newline-delimited string containing the latest board rendered with the letter-coded ARC color symbols.
- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.
- The raw numeric grid is intentionally not exposed. Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, shapes, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes. Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region; do not scan the whole board with it.
- `history` is a chronological list of action/frame snapshots.
- `history` is a Python list of objects, not a dict.
- Each history entry exposes only `.action` and `.frame`; entries are not subscriptable like `entry['action']`.
- Each `history[i].frame` is the frame after `history[i].action`; each frame exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- Important history semantics: when `history` is non-empty, `history[-1].frame` is the same latest/post-action board as `current_frame`. It is not the previous board. To inspect the state before the latest action, use `previous_frame` or `history[-2].frame` when available.
- `previous_frame` is the frame before the most recent real environment action, or `None` if no previous frame is available.
- `last_action` is the most recent real environment action name/display, or `None` before any real action.
- `last_action_frame` is the post-action frame for `last_action`; it matches `current_frame` after a real action.
- `transitions` is a chronological list of actual action transitions, excluding the initial seeded frame. Each transition exposes `.action`, `.before_frame`, `.after_frame`, `.frame` (alias of `.after_frame`), and `.result`.
- `last_transition` is `transitions[-1]` or `None`. Its `.result` mirrors `last_action_result`; older transitions may have an empty `.result`. For before/after diffs, compare `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`; do not compare `current_frame` to `history[-1].frame`.
- `last_action_result` is the persisted result dict from the most recent `action(...)` call. It remains available across later Python inspection calls that do not call `action(...)`, and is `{}` before any action result exists. Read transition metadata from fields/keys such as `last_action_result['board_changed']`, `last_action_result['done']`, `last_action_result['level_completed']`, `last_action_result['game_over']`, `last_action_result['run_complete']`, `last_action_result['reward']`, and `last_action_result['valid_actions']`.
- `valid_actions` is the current list of valid action names.
- Call `action(actions)` to execute one or more real environment actions from Python.
- Pass `action(actions)` a list like `['LEFT']` or `[{'action': 'MOUSE', 'row': 4, 'col': 7}]`.
- One action usually returns one frame, but a single action can result in a short multi-frame animation.
- After `action(actions)` returns, `current_frame`, `previous_frame`, `history`, `transitions`, `valid_actions`, and `last_action_result` are refreshed.


Multimodal context:
- User turns include an attached image of the current ARC grid.
- The image and `current_frame.ascii` are two representations of the same current frame.
- You can use images and other tools to understand the game state and guide your strategy, each may be useful depending on the current uncertainty.


Visual-game guidance:
- Treat each board as a scene with objects, blockers, targets, adjacency, containment, motion, and symmetry.
- Game entities are usually be rendered as connected multi-tile shapes such as 2×2, 2×3, 3×3, or longer patterned structures. Sometime they might also be 1x1 tokens.- Some games are logic or layout puzzles with no explicit player avatar or controllable sprite on the board. Do not assume a player exists; the relevant state may be an object, region, cursor, selector, or whole-board configuration.
- Background colors are often white or gray/black-ish large regions, but not always. Verify background hypotheses by area, stability, and object boundaries rather than assuming them.
- In many games, a long horizontal or vertical line near an edge is a timer or remaining-steps bar. It often shrinks or changes each step. If you identify such a bar, do not get distracted by it or treat it as core gameplay state unless there is concrete evidence that it interacts with the puzzle mechanics.
A common failure mode is to mistake a segmented edge bar for clickable puzzle pieces. More generally, when the objective is understood but the best action order is unclear, pathfinding, flood fill, BFS, DFS, beam search, shortest-path search, limited action-sequence search, or custom heuristics are all valid.

Runtime variables inside every `python` tool call:
- `current_frame` is a lightweight frame view for the latest environment state.
- `current_frame` exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- `current_frame.ascii` is a single newline-delimited string containing the latest board rendered with the letter-coded ARC color symbols.
- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.
- The raw numeric grid is intentionally not exposed. Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, shapes, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes. Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region; do not scan the whole board with it.
- `history` is a chronological list of action/frame snapshots.
- `history` is a Python list of objects, not a dict.
- Each history entry exposes only `.action` and `.frame`; entries are not subscriptable like `entry['action']`.
- Each `history[i].frame` is the frame after `history[i].action`; each frame exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- Important history semantics: when `history` is non-empty, `history[-1].frame` is the same latest/post-action board as `current_frame`. It is not the previous board. To inspect the state before the latest action, use `previous_frame` or `history[-2].frame` when available.
- `previous_frame` is the frame before the most recent real environment action, or `None` if no previous frame is available.
- `last_action` is the most recent real environment action name/display, or `None` before any real action.
- `last_action_frame` is the post-action frame for `last_action`; it matches `current_frame` after a real action.
- `transitions` is a chronological list of actual action transitions, excluding the initial seeded frame. Each transition exposes `.action`, `.before_frame`, `.after_frame`, `.frame` (alias of `.after_frame`), and `.result`.

<parameter=code>
import collections

# Parse the grid from ascii
ascii_lines = current_frame.ascii.split('\n')

# The grid starts at row 16, col 16 in the ascii
# Each grid cell is 3x3 pixels
# Grid is 11 rows x 11 cols (0-10)

grid = {}
for r in range(11):
    for c in range(11):
        pixel_r = 16 + r * 3
        pixel_c = 16 + c * 3
        # Sample the center of the 3x3 cell
        char = ascii_lines[pixel_r][pixel_c]
        grid[(r, c)] = char

# Print the grid
print("Grid:")
for r in range(11):
    row_str = ""
    for c in range(11):
        row_str += grid[(r, c)]
    print(f"  {r}: {row_str}")

# Build graph: from each W cell, find valid moves
# Valid move: jump over 'g' to reach 'W' or 'N' (goal)
graph = {}
for r in range(11):
    for c in range(11):
        if grid[(r, c)] == 'W':
            moves = []
            for dr, dc, direction in [(-1, 0, 'UP'), (1, 0, 'DOWN'), (0, -1, 'LEFT'), (0, 1, 'RIGHT')]:
                mid_r, mid_c = r + dr, c + dc
                target_r, target_c = r + 2*dr, c + 2*dc
                if 0 <= target_r < 11 and 0 <= target_c < 11:
                    if grid[(mid_r, mid_c)] == 'g' and grid[(target_r, target_c)] in ('W', 'N'):
                        moves.append((target_r, target_c, direction))
            graph[(r, c)] = moves

# BFS from (2, 4) to (10, 10)
start = (2, 4)
goal = (10, 10)
queue = collections.deque([(start, [start])])
visited = {start}
found_path = None

while queue:
    node, path = queue.popleft()
    if node == goal:
        found_path = path
        break
    if node in graph:
        for target, direction in graph[node]:
            if target not in visited:
                visited.add(target)
                queue.append((target, path + [target]))

if found_path:
    print(f"\nPath found: {found_path}")
    print("Path with directions:")
    for i in range(len(found_path) - 1):
        r1, c1 = found_path[i]
        r2, c2 = found_path[i + 1]
        dr = r2 - r1
        dc = c2 - c1
        if dr == -1:
            direction = 'UP'
        elif dr == 1:
            direction = 'DOWN'
        elif dc == -1:
            direction = 'LEFT'
        elif dc == 1:
            direction = 'RIGHT'
        print(f"  {direction}: ({r1}, {c1}) -> ({r2}, {c2})")
    
    # Build action sequence
    actions = []
    for i in range(len(found_path) - 1):
        r1, c1 = found_path[i]
        r2, c2 = found_path[i + 1]
        dr = r2 - r1
        dc = c2 - c1
        if dr == -1:
            actions.append('UP')
        elif dr == 1:
            actions.append('DOWN')
        elif dc == -1:
            actions.append('LEFT')
        elif dc == 1:
            actions.append('RIGHT')
    print(f"\nActions: {actions}")
else:
    print("\nNo path found!")
    
# Also check if goal (10, 10) is reachable from any W cell
print(f"\nGoal cell (10, 10): '{grid[(10, 10)]}'")
for r in range(11):
    for c in range(11):
        if grid[(r, c)] == 'W':
            for dr, dc in [(-1, 0), (1, 0), (0, -1), (0, 1)]:
                target_r, target_c = r + 2*dr, c + 2*dc
                if (target_r, target_c) == goal:
                    mid_r, mid_c = r + dr, c + dc
                    print(f"  ({r}, {c}) -> ({target_r}, {target_c}) via ({mid_r}, {mid_c})='{grid[(mid_r, mid_c)]}'")

<parameter=code>
import collections

# Parse the grid from ascii
ascii_lines = current_frame.ascii.split('\n')

# Each grid cell is 3x3 pixels, starting at pixel 16
grid = {}
for r in range(11):
    for c in range(11):
        pixel_r = 16 + r * 3
        pixel_c = 16 + c * 3
        char = ascii_lines[pixel_r][pixel_c]
        grid[(r, c)] = char

# Build graph: from each W/b cell, find valid moves
# Valid move: jump over 'g' to reach 'W', 'b', or 'N'
graph = {}
for r in range(11):
    for c in range(11):
        if grid[(r, c)] in ('W', 'b'):
            moves = []
            for dr, dc, direction in [(-1, 0, 'UP'), (1, 0, 'DOWN'), (0, -1, 'LEFT'), (0, 1, 'RIGHT')]:
                mid_r, mid_c = r + dr, c + dc
                target_r, target_c = r + 2*dr, c + 2*dc
                if 0 <= target_r < 11 and 0 <= target_c < 11:
                    if grid[(mid_r, mid_c)] == 'g' and grid[(target_r, target_c)] in ('W', 'b', 'N'):
                        moves.append((target_r, target_c, direction))
            graph[(r, c)] = moves

# BFS from (2, 4) to (10, 10)
start = (2, 4)
goal = (10, 10)
queue = collections.deque([(start, [start])])
visited = {start}
found_path = None

while queue:
    node, path = queue.popleft()
    if node == goal:
        found_path = path
        break
    if node in graph:
        for target, direction in graph[node]:
            if target not in visited:
                visited.add(target)
                queue.append((target, path + [target]))

if found_path:
    print(f"Path found: {found_path}")
    actions = []
    for i in range(len(found_path) - 1):
        r1, c1 = found_path[i]
        r2, c2 = found_path[i + 1]
        dr = r2 - r1
        dc = c2 - c1
        if dr == -1:
            actions.append('UP')
        elif dr == 1:
            actions.append('DOWN')
        elif dc == -1:
            actions.append('LEFT')
        elif dc == 1:
            actions.append('RIGHT')
    print(f"Actions ({len(actions)}): {actions}")
    
    # Execute the path
    result = action(actions)
    print(f"Result: {last_action_result}")
else:
    print("No path found!")
    # Debug: print graph for some nodes
    for node in [(2, 4), (0, 4), (4, 4), (2, 6), (6, 0), (8, 0), (8, 8), (8, 10)]:
        if node in graph:
            print(f"  {node}: {graph[node]}")
        else:
            print(f"  {node}: not in graph (grid value: '{grid[node]}')")

import collections

# Parse the grid from ascii
ascii_lines = current_frame.ascii.split('\n')

grid = {}
for r in range(11):
    for c in range(11):
        pixel_r = 16 + r * 3
        pixel_c = 16 + c * 3
        char = ascii_lines[pixel_r][pixel_c]
        grid[(r, c)] = char

# Build graph
graph = {}
for r in range(11):
    for c in range(11):
        if grid[(r, c)] in ('W', 'b'):
            moves = []
            for dr, dc, direction in [(-1, 0, 'UP'), (1, 0, 'DOWN'), (0, -1, 'LEFT'), (0, 1, 'RIGHT')]:
                mid_r, mid_c = r + dr, c + dc
                target_r, target_c = r + 2*dr, c + 2*dc
                if 0 <= target_r < 11 and 0 <= target_c < 11:
                    if grid[(mid_r, mid_c)] == 'g' and grid[(target_r, target_c)] in ('W', 'b', 'N'):
                        moves.append(((target_r, target_c), direction))
            graph[(r, c)] = moves

# BFS from (2, 4) to (10, 10)
start = (2, 4)
goal = (10, 10)
queue = collections.deque([(start, [start])])
visited = {start}
found_path = None

while queue:
    node, path = queue.popleft()
    if node == goal:
        found_path = path
        break
    if node in graph:
        for target, direction in graph[node]:
            if target not in visited:
                visited.add(target)
                queue.append((target, path + [target]))

if found_path:
    print(f"Path found ({len(found_path)} steps): {found_path}")
    actions = []
    for i in range(len(found_path) - 1):
        r1, c1 = found_path[i]
        r2, c2 = found_path[i + 1]
        dr = r2 - r1
        dc = c2 - c1
        if dr == -1:
            actions.append('UP')
        elif dr == 1:
            actions.append('DOWN')
        elif dc == -1:
            actions.append('LEFT')
        elif dc == 1:
            actions.append('RIGHT')
    print(f"Actions ({len(actions)}): {actions}")
    result = action(actions)
    print(f"Result: done={last_action_result.get('done')}, level_completed={last_action_result.get('level_completed')}")
else:
    print("No path found!")
    for node in [(2,4), (0,4), (4,4), (2,6), (6,0), (8,0), (8,8), (8,10)]:
        if node in graph:
            print(f"  {node}: {graph[node]}")
        else:
            print(f"  {node}: not in graph (grid='{grid[node]}')")

import collections

# Parse the grid from ascii
ascii_lines = current_frame.ascii.split('\n')

# Find the grid boundaries by looking for non-B characters
grid_rows = []
for r in range(64):
    row_chars = []
    for c in range(64):
        char = ascii_lines[r][c]
        if char != 'B':
            row_chars.append((c, char))
    if row_chars:
        grid_rows.append((r, row_chars))

print(f"Non-black rows: {len(grid_rows)}")

    r = (min(p[0] for p in b) + max(p[0] for p in b)) // 2
    c = (min(p[1] for p in b) + max(p[1] for p in b)) // 2
    if n['color'] == 'b':
        player_pos = (r, c)
        print(f"Player at pixel ({r}, {c})")
    elif n['color'] == 'N':
        goal_pos = (r, c)
        print(f"Goal at pixel ({r}, {c})")

# Parse the grid - find the row with most non-B characters
# The grid seems to be a horizontal strip
# Let's find the row range
all_rows = set()
for n in nodes:
    if n['color'] not in ('B',):
        b = n['boundary']
        min_r = min(p[0] for p in b)
        max_r = max(p[0] for p in b)
        for r in range(min_r, max_r + 1):
            all_rows.add(r)

for n in nodes:

        b = n['boundary']
        min_c = min(p[1] for p in b)
        max_c = max(p[1] for p in b)

            all_cols.add(c)

<function=python>
<parameter=code>
import collections

seg = current_frame.segmentation
nodes = seg['nodes']
adj = seg['adjacency_list']

# Find player, goal, W nodes, g nodes
player_id = None
goal_id = None
w_nodes = {}
g_nodes = {}

for n in nodes:
    b = n['boundary']
    r = (min(p[0] for p in b) + max(p[0] for p in b)) // 2
    c = (min(p[1] for p in b) + max(p[1] for p in b)) // 2
    if n['color'] == 'b':
        player_id = n['id']
        player_pos = (r, c)
        print(f"Player: id={n['id']}, pixel=({r}, {c})")
    elif n['color'] == 'N':
        goal_id = n['id']
        goal_pos = (r, c)
        print(f"Goal: id={n['id']}, pixel=({r}, {c})")
    elif n['color'] == 'W':
        w_nodes[n['id']] = (r, c)
    elif n['color'] == 'g':
        g_nodes[n['id']] = (r, c)
    elif n['color'] == 'R':
        print(f"Red node: id={n['id']}, pixel=({r}, {c})")

print(f"\nW nodes: {len(w_nodes)}")
print(f"g nodes: {len(g_nodes)}")

# Build graph: connect W nodes (and player/goal) through g nodes
# For each g node, find its non-g neighbors and connect them
graph = collections.defaultdict(set)
for g_id in g_nodes:
    neighbors = []
    for a, b in adj:
        if a == g_id:
            if nodes[b]['color'] not in ('g', 'B'):
                neighbors.append(b)
        elif b == g_id:
            if nodes[a]['color'] not in ('g', 'B'):
                neighbors.append(a)
    for i in range(len(neighbors)):
        for j in range(i+1, len(neighbors)):
            graph[neighbors[i]].add(neighbors[j])
            graph[neighbors[j]].add(neighbors[i])

# BFS from player to goal
start = player_id
goal = goal_id
queue = collections.deque([(start, [start])])
visited = {start}
found_path = None

while queue:
    node, path = queue.popleft()
    if node == goal:
        found_path = path
        break
    for neighbor in graph[node]:
        if neighbor not in visited:
            visited.add(neighbor)
            queue.append((neighbor, path + [neighbor]))

if found_path:
    print(f"\nPath found ({len(found_path)} nodes): {found_path}")
    # Determine directions
    actions = []
    for i in range(len(found_path) - 1):
        nid1 = found_path[i]
        nid2 = found_path[i + 1]
        # Get positions
        if nid1 == player_id:
            pos1 = player_pos
        elif nid1 == goal_id:
            pos1 = goal_pos
        elif nid1 in w_nodes:
            pos1 = w_nodes[nid1]
        elif nid1 in g_nodes:
            pos1 = g_nodes[nid1]
        else:
            pos1 = (0, 0)
        
        if nid2 == player_id:
            pos2 = player_pos
        elif nid2 == goal_id:
            pos2 = goal_pos
        elif nid2 in w_nodes:
            pos2 = w_nodes[nid2]
        elif nid2 in g_nodes:
            pos2 = g_nodes[nid2]
        else:
            pos2 = (0, 0)
        
        dr = pos2[0] - pos1[0]
        dc = pos2[1] - pos1[1]
        if dr < 0:
            actions.append('UP')
        elif dr > 0:
            actions.append('DOWN')
        elif dc < 0:
            actions.append('LEFT')
        elif dc > 0:
            actions.append('RIGHT')
    
    print(f"Actions ({len(actions)}): {actions}")
    result = action(actions)
    print(f"Result: done={last_action_result.get('done')}, level_completed={last_action_result.get('level_completed')}")
else:
    print("\nNo path found!")
    # Debug
    print(f"Player neighbors: {graph[player_id]}")
    print(f"Goal neighbors: {graph[goal_id]}")

Game overview:
- You are solving a multi-level grid puzzle game. 
- You are called repeatedly over the course of a run. Treat each turn as one observe-plan-act cycle: re-understand the current state from the newest frame, update your working world model in Python, choose the next best action or short sequence against the goal as currently understood, execute it, and expect to re-evaluate on the next turn from the updated state.
- Your job is to solve the entire game by clearing every level, not just the current screen.
- Levels often build on earlier mechanics, but layouts and interactions can still change between levels.
- Optimize for as few in-game actions as possible while still being reliable.
- In this environment, boards are presented as 64 x 64 color grids rendered with ARC color symbols.
- Color legend: W=white, w=light gray, g=gray, G=dark gray, c=charcoal, B=black, M=magenta, P=pink, R=red, b=blue, S=sky blue, Y=yellow, O=orange, r=dark red, N=light green, p=purple.


Runtime variables inside every `python` tool call:
- `current_frame` is a lightweight frame view for the latest environment state.
- `current_frame` exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- `current_frame.ascii` is a single newline-delimited string containing the latest board rendered with the letter-coded ARC color symbols.
- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.
- The raw numeric grid is intentionally not exposed. Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, shapes, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes. Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region; do not scan the whole board with it.
- `history` is a chronological list of action/frame snapshots.
- `history` is a Python list of objects, not a dict.
- Each history entry exposes only `.action` and `.frame`; entries are not subscriptable like `entry['action']`.
- Each `history[i].frame` is the frame after `history[i].action`; each frame exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- Important history semantics: when `history` is non-empty, `history[-1].frame` is the same latest/post-action board as `current_frame`. It is not the previous board. To inspect the state before the latest action, use `previous_frame` or `history[-2].frame` when available.
- `previous_frame` is the frame before the most recent real environment action, or `None` if no previous frame is available.
- `last_action` is the most recent real environment action name/display, or `None` before any real action.
- `last_action_frame` is the post-action frame for `last_action`; it matches `current_frame` after a real action.
- `transitions` is a chronological list of actual action transitions, excluding the initial seeded frame. Each transition exposes `.action`, `.before_frame`, `.after_frame`, `.frame` (alias of `.after_frame`), and `.result`.
- `last_transition` is `transitions[-1]` or `None`. Its `.result` mirrors `last_action_result`; older transitions may have an empty `.result`. For before/after diffs, compare `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`; do not compare `current_frame` to `history[-1].frame`.
- `last_action_result` is the persisted result dict from the most recent `action(...)` call. It remains available across later Python inspection calls that do not call `action(...)`, and is `{}` before any action result exists. Read transition metadata from fields/keys such as `last_action_result['board_changed']`, `last_action_result['done']`, `last_action_result['level_completed']`, `last_action_result['game_over']`, `last_action_result['run_complete']`, `last_action_result['reward']`, and `last_action_result['valid_actions']`.
- `valid_actions` is the current list of valid action names.
- Call `action(actions)` to execute one or more real environment actions from Python.
- Pass `action(actions)` a list like `['LEFT']` or `[{'action': 'MOUSE', 'row': 4, 'col': 7}]`.
- One action usually returns one frame, but a single action can result in a short multi-frame animation.
- After `action(actions)` returns, `current_frame`, `previous_frame`, `history`, `transitions`, `valid_actions`, and `last_action_result` are refreshed.


Multimodal context:
- User turns include an attached image of the current ARC grid.
- The image and `current_frame.ascii` are two representations of the same current frame.
- You can use images and other tools to understand the game state and guide your strategy, each may be useful depending on the current uncertainty.


Visual-game guidance:
- Treat each board as a scene with objects, blockers, targets, adjacency, containment, motion, and symmetry.
- Game entities are usually be rendered as connected multi-tile shapes such as 2×2, 2×3, 3×3, or longer patterned structures. Sometime they might also be 1x1 tokens.- Some games are logic or layout puzzles with no explicit player avatar or controllable sprite on the board. Do not assume a player exists; the relevant state may be an object, region, cursor, selector, or whole-board configuration.
- Background colors are often white or gray/black-ish large regions, but not always. Verify background hypotheses by area, stability, and object boundaries rather than assuming them.
- In many games, a long horizontal or vertical line near an edge is a timer or remaining-steps bar. It often shrinks or changes each step. If you identify such a bar, do not get distracted by it or treat it as core gameplay state unless there is concrete evidence that it interacts with the puzzle mechanics.
A common failure mode is to mistake a segmented edge bar for clickable puzzle pieces. If a repeated strip of small blocks sits flush against the top, bottom, left, or right border and actions only change that strip while the interior board stays the same, classify it as HUD/timer state, not as an object to click through segment by segment. DON'T DO THIS!
- Use coordinates only to target actions or describe local evidence. Do not frame the objective as reaching a specific absolute row or column.
- Re-ground on the newest frame after any score increase or abrupt scene change; the returned board may already be the next level.
- `WIN` means the whole game is solved. Mid-run level completion is more likely to appear as a score increase while play continues.
- Strategies may transfer loosely across levels, but layouts and mechanics can change. Re-check the new board before repeating a plan.
- For `MOUSE`, pass `row` and `col` integer arguments. `row` is vertical position, `col` is horizontal position.


Python tool guidance:
- Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes.
- Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region of the board when `segmentation` is not enough; never use it to scan or summarize the whole board.
- Every `python` tool call starts fresh. Re-import modules or re-define any custom utility logic you need.
- The only importable standard-library modules are: bisect, collections, copy, fractions, functools, heapq, itertools, json, math, operator, random, re, statistics, string.
- The only tool is `python`; call it with one ephemeral `code` string.
- Always inspect `current_frame`, `history`, and `valid_actions` from Python instead of reasoning from the raw board by eye.
- For the most recent change, compare `previous_frame` to `current_frame`, or `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`. `history[-1].frame` is the current frame, so comparing it to `current_frame` only compares the board to itself.
- Maintain a compact working world model: what entities or regions exist, what actions seem to do, what the goal likely is, what remains uncertain, and what plan best fits the evidence so far.
- IMPORTANT: Especially when the game is about making an agent navigate to a target, it is usually safer to write an explicit search algorithm such as BFS. More generally, when the objective is understood but the best action order is unclear, pathfinding, flood fill, BFS, DFS, beam search, shortest-path search, limited action-sequence search, or custom heuristics are all valid.
- Optimize for the shortest reliable sequence that advances the current goal as described by your world model. If confidence is low, program a discriminating probe and revise the world model from the result.
- Once the important state variables and action effects are sufficiently understood, stop probing and search in the inferred state space.
- Inspect current and history frames from Python instead of describing frames freehand.
- Never print or echo full board frames. Return only compact derived summaries such as object lists, diffs, coordinates, counts, or tiny local crops.
- Keep tool-output context size minimal and decision-oriented so you can quickly compare before/after state. It's fine to write a lot of python code, just make the output short and interpretable
- A strong default loop is: summarize the board, infer the desired environment change, write a small scorer or search over candidate sequences, execute the best probe or plan with `action(...)`, then inspect again until you understand exactly what changed.
- For object tracking, match objects by color, overlap, bounding box proximity, area change, and edge contact rather than by exact coordinates alone.
- For frame diffs, summarize changed cells, color transitions, appearing/disappearing components, movement candidates, and small local row slices around the changed region.
- After every action, verify whether gameplay objects changed or whether only a timer, progress bar, or remaining-step bar moved. Do not treat HUD-only changes as evidence that the move worked.
- Use `print(...)` for compact summaries, or assign a final compact object to `result`.
- Call `action(...)` inside Python rather than returning action text in the chat.
- `action(...)` accepts an ordered list of one or more actions. Once your code has selected a reliable sequence, it is often useful to batch it.
- You can also call `action(...)` multiple times in one Python snippet, including inside loops. Each call updates the preloaded variables before execution continues.
- If an action result reports `game_over`, `run_complete`, `level_completed`, or `done`, stop acting immediately and re-ground on the next turn.

# Check current state - did we hit the red trap?
seg = current_frame.segmentation
nodes = seg['nodes']

player_pos = None
goal_pos = None
for n in nodes:
    b = n['boundary']
    r = (min(p[0] for p in b) + max(p[0] for p in b)) // 2
    c = (min(p[1] for p in b) + max(p[1] for p in b)) // 2
    if n['color'] == 'b':
        player_pos = (r, c)
        print(f"Player at pixel ({r}, {c}), id={n['id']}")
    elif n['color'] == 'N':
        goal_pos = (r, c)
        print(f"Goal at pixel ({r}, {c}), id={n['id']}")
    elif n['color'] == 'R':
        print(f"Red node at pixel ({r}, {c}), id={n['id']}")

print(f"\nLast action result: {last_action_result}")
print(f"Valid actions: {valid_actions}")

# The path went through the red node (id=11), which is likely a trap
# Need to find a path that avoids the red node
# Let me rebuild the graph excluding the red node
import collections

adj = seg['adjacency_list']
w_nodes = {}
g_nodes = {}
red_id = None
player_id = None
goal_id = None

for n in nodes:
    b = n['boundary']
    r = (min(p[0] for p in b) + max(p[0] for p in b)) // 2
    c = (min(p[1] for p in b) + max(p[1] for p in b)) // 2
    if n['color'] == 'b':
        player_id = n['id']
    elif n['color'] == 'N':
        goal_id = n['id']
    elif n['color'] == 'W':
        w_nodes[n['id']] = (r, c)
    elif n['color'] == 'g':
        g_nodes[n['id']] = (r, c)
    elif n['color'] == 'R':
        red_id = n['id']

print(f"\nRed node id: {red_id}")

# Build graph excluding red node
graph = collections.defaultdict(set)
for g_id in g_nodes:
    neighbors = []
    for a, b in adj:
        if a == g_id:
            if nodes[b]['color'] not in ('g', 'B', 'R'):
                neighbors.append(b)
        elif b == g_id:
            if nodes[a]['color'] not in ('g', 'B', 'R'):
                neighbors.append(a)
    for i in range(len(neighbors)):
        for j in range(i+1, len(neighbors)):
            graph[neighbors[i]].add(neighbors[j])
            graph[neighbors[j]].add(neighbors[i])

# BFS from player to goal, avoiding red node
start = player_id
goal = goal_id
queue = collections.deque([(start, [start])])
visited = {start}
found_path = None

while queue:
    node, path = queue.popleft()
    if node == goal:
        found_path = path
        break
    for neighbor in graph[node]:
        if neighbor not in visited and neighbor != red_id:
            visited.add(neighbor)
            queue.append((neighbor, path + [neighbor]))

if found_path:
    print(f"\nPath found (avoiding red): {found_path}")
    actions = []
    for i in range(len(found_path) - 1):
        nid1 = found_path[i]
        nid2 = found_path[i + 1]
        # Get positions
        pos1 = pos2 = None
        for nid, pos_dict in [(nid1, {'player': player_pos, 'goal': goal_pos}), (nid2, {'player': player_pos, 'goal': goal_pos})]:
            pass
        # Simpler: get from nodes
        n1 = next(n for n in nodes if n['id'] == nid1)
        n2 = next(n for n in nodes if n['id'] == nid2)
        b1 = n1['boundary']
        b2 = n2['boundary']
        r1 = (min(p[0] for p in b1) + max(p[0] for p in b1)) // 2
        c1 = (min(p[1] for p in b1) + max(p[1] for p in b1)) // 2
        r2 = (min(p[0] for p in b2) + max(p[0] for p in b2)) // 2
        c2 = (min(p[1] for p in b2) + max(p[1] for p in b2)) // 2
        dr = r2 - r1
        dc = c2 - c1
        if dr < 0:
            actions.append('UP')
        elif dr > 0:
            actions.append('DOWN')
        elif dc < 0:
            actions.append('LEFT')
        elif dc > 0:
            actions.append('RIGHT')
    print(f"Actions: {actions}")
else:
    print("\nNo path found avoiding red node!")

import collections

seg = current_frame.segmentation
nodes = seg['nodes']
adj = seg['adjacency_list']

# Find all nodes
player_id = None
goal_id = None
red_id = None
w_nodes = {}
g_nodes = {}

for n in nodes:
    b = n['boundary']
    r = (min(p[0] for p in b) + max(p[0] for p in b)) // 2
    c = (min(p[1] for p in b) + max(p[1] for p in b)) // 2
    if n['color'] == 'b':
        player_id = n['id']
        player_pos = (r, c)
    elif n['color'] == 'N':
        goal_id = n['id']
        goal_pos = (r, c)
    elif n['color'] == 'W':
        w_nodes[n['id']] = (r, c)
    elif n['color'] == 'g':
        g_nodes[n['id']] = (r, c)
    elif n['color'] == 'R':
        red_id = n['id']
        red_pos = (r, c)

print(f"Player: id={player_id}, pos={player_pos}")
print(f"Goal: id={goal_id}, pos={goal_pos}")
print(f"Red: id={red_id}, pos={red_pos}")
print(f"W nodes: {len(w_nodes)}, g nodes: {len(g_nodes)}")

# Build graph: connect nodes through g nodes
graph = collections.defaultdict(set)
for g_id in g_nodes:
    neighbors = []
    for a, b in adj:
        if a == g_id:
            nid = b
            if nodes[nid]['color'] not in ('g', 'B'):
                neighbors.append(nid)
        elif b == g_id:
            nid = a
            if nodes[nid]['color'] not in ('g', 'B'):
                neighbors.append(nid)
    for i in range(len(neighbors)):
        for j in range(i+1, len(neighbors)):
            graph[neighbors[i]].add(neighbors[j])
            graph[neighbors[j]].add(neighbors[i])

# Print graph for debugging
print("\nGraph connections:")
for nid in sorted(graph.keys()):
    color = nodes[nid]['color']
    print(f"  {nid} ({color}): {sorted(graph[nid])}")

# BFS from player to goal, avoiding red node
start = player_id
goal = goal_id
queue = collections.deque([(start, [start])])
visited = {start}
found_path = None

while queue:
    node, path = queue.popleft()
    if node == goal:
        found_path = path
        break
    for neighbor in graph[node]:
        if neighbor not in visited and neighbor != red_id:
            visited.add(neighbor)
            queue.append((neighbor, path + [neighbor]))

if found_path:
    print(f"\nPath found (avoiding red): {found_path}")
    actions = []
    for i in range(len(found_path) - 1):
        nid1 = found_path[i]
        nid2 = found_path[i + 1]
        n1 = next(n for n in nodes if n['id'] == nid1)
        n2 = next(n for n in nodes if n['id'] == nid2)
        b1 = n1['boundary']
        b2 = n2['boundary']
        r1 = (min(p[0] for p in b1) + max(p[0] for p in b1)) // 2
        c1 = (min(p[1] for p in b1) + max(p[1] for p in b1)) // 2
        r2 = (min(p[0] for p in b2) + max(p[0] for p in b2)) // 2
        c2 = (min(p[1] for p in b2) + max(p[1] for p in b2)) // 2
        dr = r2 - r1
        dc = c2 - c1
        if dr < 0:
            actions.append('UP')
        elif dr > 0:
            actions.append('DOWN')
        elif dc < 0:
            actions.append('LEFT')
        elif dc > 0:
            actions.append('RIGHT')
    print(f"Actions: {actions}")
    result = action(actions)
    print(f"Result: done={last_action_result.get('done')}, level_completed={last_action_result.get('level_completed')}")
else:
    print("\nNo path found avoiding red node!")
    # Try including red node
    queue = collections.deque([(start, [start])])
    visited = {start}
    found_path = None
    while queue:
        node, path = queue.popleft()
        if node == goal:
            found_path = path
            break
        for neighbor in graph[node]:
            if neighbor not in visited:
                visited.add(neighbor)
                queue.append((neighbor, path + [neighbor]))
    if found_path:
        print(f"Path through red: {found_path}")
    else:
        print("No path at all!")
